It is undeniable that modern neural networks (NNs) are becoming larger and more complex, with many state-of-the-art models now employing billions of trainable parameters [1, 2, 3]. While parameter count may be a crude way of quantifying complexity, there is little doubt that these models have enormous capacity, often far more than is needed to perfectly fit the training data, even if the labels are pure noise [4]. Surprisingly, these same high-capacity models generalize well when trained on real data.

These observations conflict with classical generalization theory, which contends that models of intermediate complexity should generalize best, striking a balance between the bias and the variance of their predictive functions. A paradigm for understanding the observed generalization behavior of modern methods is known as double descent [5], in which the test error behaves as predicted by classical theory and follows the standard U-shaped curve until the point where the training set can be fit exactly, but after this point it begins to descend again, eventually finding its global minimum in the overparameterized regime.

While double descent has been the focus of significant research, a concrete and interpretable theoretical explanation for the phenomenon has thus far been lacking. One of the challenges in developing such an explanation is that the full phenomenology of double descent is not evident in linear models that are easy to analyze. Indeed, for linear models the number of parameters is tied to the number of features and there is no natural way to adjust the capacity of the model without simultaneously adjusting the data distribution. In this work, we overcome this challenge by providing a precise asymptotic analysis of random feature kernel regression, which is a model rich enough to exhibit all the interesting features of double descent.

Another challenge in understanding double descent is that the classical bias-variance decomposition is itself insufficiently nuanced to reveal all the underlying explanatory factors. Indeed, modern learning algorithms typically involve multiple sources of randomness and isolating the variation caused by each of these sources of randomness is key to building an effective interpretation. As we will see, it is not possible to fully understand the spike in test error near the interpolation threshold without performing a truly multivariate variance decomposition.

While decomposing the variance has been proposed before, prior work has naively relied on the law of total variance, which requires specifying an ordering of conditioning that leads to some arbitrariness. Instead, we present a principled symmetric decomposition which leads to unambiguous interpretations and clear credit assignment. Decomposing the variance of a random variable in this way is related to ANOVA [6], which has been used previously in a machine learning context to find the best approximating functions (in terms of mean squared error) to a random variable with limited dependence on the inputs [7, 8] and to study quasi Monte Carlo methods for integration [9].

Finally, we remark that an improved understanding of the bias and variance of machine learning models might naturally suggest ways to improve their performance. Specifically, any prior knowledge about what sources of variance may be dominant could help inform decisions about which types of ensemble or bagging techniques to utilize.

The idea of a trade-off between bias and variance has a long history, with theoretical and experimental support having been well established in a variety of contexts over the years. The seminal paper of Geman et al. [10] examines a number of models, ranging from kernel regression to k𝑘k-nearest neighbor to neural networks, and concludes that the trade-off exists in all cases111Interestingly, the variance of simple feed-forward neural networks was observed to eventually be a decreasing function of width, but the authors rationalized this early evidence of double descent as a quirk of the optimization.. The resulting U-shaped test error curve was verified theoretically in a variety of classical settings, see e.g. [11].

In recent years, these conclusions have been called into question by the intriguing experimental results of [4, 12], which were later replicated in a number of settings, see e.g. [13], which showed that deep neural networks and kernel methods can generalize well even in the interpolation regime, implying that both the bias and the variance can decrease as the model complexity increases. A number of theoretical results have since established this behavior in certain settings, such as interpolating nearest neighbor schemes [14] and kernel regression [15, 16]. These observations have given rise to the double descent paradigm for understanding how test error depends on model complexity [5]. The influential work [17] (which actually predates [5]) established initial theoretical insights for linear networks and found empirical evidence of double descent for nonlinear networks; more evidence has followed recently in [13, 18]. Precise theoretical predictions soon confirmed this picture for linear regression in various scenarios [19, 20, 21, 22], and recently even for kernel regression [23, 24] with random features related to neural networks.

The primary focus of these recent works has been on double descent in the total test error, or perhaps the standard bias-variance decomposition with respect to label noise [23]. A multivariate philosophy similar to ours is advanced in [25], which revisited the empirical study of the bias-variance tradeoff in neural networks from [10] and showed the variance can decrease in the overparameterized regime. However, in that work the variance is simply decomposed using the law of total variance, which, while mathematically sound, can lead to ambiguous conclusions, as we discuss in Sec. 4.

The main mathematical tools we utilize come from random matrix theory and build on the results of [26, 27, 28, 29, 30] for studying random matrices with nonlinear dependencies. We also rely on techniques from operator-valued free probability for computing traces of large block matrices [31]. One advantage of these tools is that they facilitate the extension of our analysis to more general settings, including the case of kernel regression with respect to the Neural Tangent Kernel (NTK) [32]. To ease the exposition we have deferred the discussion of the NTK and all proofs to the Supplementary Material (SM).

While finalizing this manuscript, we became aware of several concurrent works that examine similar questions. Yang et al. [33] define the total bias and variance similarly to [25], but they do not attempt a decomposition of the variance. Their results can be derived as a special case of our fine-grained decomposition by summing the variance terms in Thm. 1. Jacot et al. [34] study the relationship between the random feature model and the nonparametric Gaussian process which it approximates. The bias-variance decomposition considered in that paper is again univariate and is with respect to the randomness in the random features (the expressions are subsequently averaged over the training data). Closest to our work is [35], which also studies a multivariate decomposition of the random feature model in the high-dimensional limit. Unlike our approach, their decomposition is not symmetric with respect to the underlying random variables, and the results depend on the chosen order of conditioning. Their particular choice, and indeed all possible choices, arise as special cases of our general result. See Sec. S8 for a detailed discussion.

1.We develop a symmetric, interpretable variance decomposition suitable for modern deep learning algorithms2.We compute this decomposition analytically for random feature kernel regression in the high-dimensional asymptotic regime3.We prove that the bias is monotonically decreasing as the width increases and that it is finite at the interpolation threshold4.We clarify the relationship between label noise and double descent: while the test loss can diverge at the interpolation threshold without label noise, the divergence is exacerbated by it5.We provide a quantitative description of how both ensemble and bagging methods can eliminate double descent, since the divergence is caused by variance terms due to the interactions between sampling and initialization

In this section, we trace through the evolution of several ways to analyze the bias-variance trade-off. By analyzing their shortcomings, we motivate our fine-grained analysis that follows.

The bias-variance trade-off has long served as a useful paradigm for understanding the generalization of machine learning algorithms. For a given test point 𝐱𝐱\mathbf{x}, it decomposes the expected error as𝔼​[y^​(𝐱)−y​(𝐱)]2=(𝔼​y^​(𝐱)−𝔼​y​(𝐱))2+𝕍​[y^​(𝐱)]+𝕍​[y​(𝐱)],𝔼superscriptdelimited-[]^𝑦𝐱𝑦𝐱2superscript𝔼^𝑦𝐱𝔼𝑦𝐱2𝕍delimited-[]^𝑦𝐱𝕍delimited-[]𝑦𝐱\mathbb{E}\left[{\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})-y(\mathbf{x})}\right]^{2}=\left({\mathbb{E}\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})-\mathbb{E}y(\mathbf{x})}\right)^{2}+\mathbb{V}\left[{\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})}\right]+\mathbb{V}[{y(\mathbf{x})}]\,,(1)and subsequently averages over the test point to obtain a decomposition of the test error in which the first term is the bias, the second term is the variance, and the third term is the irreducible noise. In classical settings, the randomness of the predictive function is usually regarded as coming from randomness in the training data, i.e. sampling noise. This leads to two common conventions, where the expectations in eqn. (1) are over both X𝑋X and 𝐲𝐲\mathbf{y} or are conditional on X𝑋X and only over the label noise in 𝐲𝐲\mathbf{y}. For concreteness and to simplify the exposition, in this subsection we adopt the latter convention and make the common modelling assumption that the sampling noise is an additive term 𝜺𝜺\boldsymbol{\varepsilon} on the training labels but is zero on the test labels y​(𝐱)𝑦𝐱y(\mathbf{x}). Using 𝔼𝐱subscript𝔼𝐱\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}} to denote expectation over the test point, we haveEtest:=𝔼𝐱𝔼𝜺[y^(𝐱)−y(𝐱)]2=𝔼𝐱​(𝔼𝜺​[y^​(𝐱)]−y​(𝐱))2⏟Bias+𝔼𝐱​𝕍𝜺​[y^​(𝐱)]⏟Variance.E_{\text{test}}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}\left[{\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})-y(\mathbf{x})}\right]^{2}=\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\left({\mathbb{E}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}[\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})]-y(\mathbf{x})}\right)^{2}}_{\text{Bias}}+\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{V}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}\left[{\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})}\right]}_{\text{Variance}}\,.(2)We refer to eqn. (2) as the classical bias-variance decomposition.

Modern methods for training neural networks often utilize additional sources of randomness, such as the initial parameter values, minibatch selection, etc., which we collectively denote by θ𝜃\mathbf{\theta}. One is therefore left with a choice regarding whether or not to include θ𝜃\mathbf{\theta} in the expectations in eqn. (1), or to simply average over θ𝜃\mathbf{\theta} when computing the test loss. We explore the ramifications of these different choices below.

In what we call the semi-classical approach, the additional random variables θ𝜃\mathbf{\theta} coming from initialization or optimization are not included in the expectations in eqn. (1); we instead average over these quantities to defineEtest:=𝔼𝐱𝔼θ𝔼𝜺[(y^(𝐱)−y(𝐱))2|θ]=𝔼𝐱​𝔼θ​(𝔼𝜺​[y^​(𝐱)|θ]−y​(𝐱))2⏟BS​C+𝔼𝐱​𝔼θ​𝕍𝜺​[y^​(𝐱)|θ]⏟VS​C.E_{\text{test}}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{\theta}}\mathbb{E}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}[(\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})-y(\mathbf{x}))^{2}|\mathbf{\theta}]=\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{\theta}}\left({\mathbb{E}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}[\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})|\mathbf{\theta}]-y(\mathbf{x})}\right)^{2}}_{B_{SC}}+\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{\theta}}\mathbb{V}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}[\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})|\mathbf{\theta}]}_{V_{SC}}\,.(3)In some scenarios, such as the high-dimensional setup analyzed in [23], the additional averaging over θ𝜃\theta is unnecessary as the distributions concentrate around their mean. In those situations, the semi-classical decomposition is identical to the classical one, thus motivating this particular approach.

In what we call the multivariate approach, the additional random variables θ𝜃\mathbf{\theta} are included in the expectations in eqn. (1), so that all random variables are on the same footing. We can then drop explicit references to θ𝜃\mathbf{\theta} and 𝜺𝜺\boldsymbol{\varepsilon} and simply write,Etest:=𝔼𝐱𝔼(y^(𝐱)−y(𝐱))2=𝔼𝐱​(𝔼​[y^​(𝐱)]−y​(𝐱))2⏟B+𝔼𝐱​𝕍​[y^​(𝐱)]⏟V.E_{\text{test}}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}(\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})-y(\mathbf{x}))^{2}=\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\left({\mathbb{E}[\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})]-y(\mathbf{x})}\right)^{2}}_{B}+\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{V}[\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})]}_{V}\,.(4)One advantage of this perspective is that its form is completely symmetric with respect to the underlying random variables. Another is that the predictive function y^​(𝐱)^𝑦𝐱\hat{y}(\mathbf{x}) appearing in the bias B𝐵B is not conditional on any random variables. As we discuss in Sec. 4, this facilitates its interpretation as a measure of erroneous assumptions in the model.

The downside of this perspective is that the variance V𝑉V no longer admits a simple interpretation since it contains contributions from multiple random variables. This problem can be remedied by further decomposing the variance.

To gain further insight into the structure of the total variance V𝑉V and how individual random variables contribute to it, it can be useful to write V𝑉V as a sum of individual terms, each with an unambiguous meaning.

One path forward is to rely on the law of total variance: 𝕍​[𝒴]=𝔼​𝕍​[𝒴|𝒳]+𝕍​𝔼​[𝒴|𝒳]𝕍delimited-[]𝒴𝔼𝕍delimited-[]conditional𝒴𝒳𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝒴𝒳\mathbb{V}\left[{\mathcal{Y}}\right]=\mathbb{E}\mathbb{V}\left[{\mathcal{Y}|\mathcal{X}}\right]+\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}\left[{\mathcal{Y}|\mathcal{X}}\right], where the terms represent the variance of 𝒴𝒴\mathcal{Y} unexplained and explained by 𝒳𝒳\mathcal{X} respectively. However, one is immediately confronted by the question of which source of randomness to condition on. As we discuss in Sec. 4.2, different choices yield different terms and can lead to ambiguous interpretations.

To avoid this ambiguity, we introduce a fully-symmetric decomposition, which turns out to be unique if we additionally require self-consistency under marginalization with respect to all variables.

Following prior work modeling double descent [21, 23, 24], we perform our analysis in the high-dimensional asymptotic scaling limit in which the dataset size m𝑚m, feature dimensionality n0subscript𝑛0n_{0}, and hidden layer size n1subscript𝑛1n_{1} all tend to infinity at the same rate, with ϕ:=n0/m\phi\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=n_{0}/m and ψ:=n0/n1\psi\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=n_{0}/n_{1} held constant.

We consider the task of learning an unknown function from m𝑚m independent samples (𝐱i,yi)∈ℝn0×ℝ,i=1,…,mformulae-sequencesubscript𝐱𝑖subscript𝑦𝑖superscriptℝsubscript𝑛0ℝ𝑖1…𝑚(\mathbf{x}_{i},y_{i})\in\mathbb{R}^{n_{0}}\times\mathbb{R},\,i=1,\ldots,m, where the datapoints are standard Gaussian, 𝐱i∼𝒩​(0,In0)similar-tosubscript𝐱𝑖𝒩0subscript𝐼subscript𝑛0\mathbf{x}_{i}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,I_{n_{0}}), and the labels are generated by a linear function parameterized by β∈ℝn0𝛽superscriptℝsubscript𝑛0\beta\in\mathbb{R}^{n_{0}}, whose entries are drawn independently from 𝒩​(0,1)𝒩01\mathcal{N}(0,1). Although this may seem like a simple data distribution, it turns out that, in these high-dimensional asymptotics, the much more general setting in which the labels are produced by a non-linear teacher neural network can be exactly modeled with a linear teacher of this form (see Sec. S2.1).

Altogether, the test loss can be written asEtest=𝔼β​𝔼𝐱​(y​(𝐱)−y^​(𝐱))2=𝔼𝐱​(β⊤​𝐱/n0−Y​K−1​K𝐱)2,subscript𝐸testsubscript𝔼𝛽subscript𝔼𝐱superscript𝑦𝐱^𝑦𝐱2subscript𝔼𝐱superscriptsuperscript𝛽top𝐱subscript𝑛0𝑌superscript𝐾1subscript𝐾𝐱2E_{\text{test}}=\mathbb{E}_{\beta}\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}(y(\mathbf{x})-\hat{y}(\mathbf{x}))^{2}=\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}(\beta^{\top}\mathbf{x}/\sqrt{n_{0}}-YK^{-1}K_{\mathbf{x}})^{2}\,,(20)where we dropped the outer expectation over β𝛽\beta because the distribution concentrates around its mean (see the SM).

The fine-grained variance decomposition given in Thm. 1 provides a powerful tool for understanding the origins of double descent. In this section, we use this tool to reinterpret several counterintuitive observations made in prior work and to provide a clear and unambiguous characterization of the source of double descent.

In [21, 23], double descent in random feature kernel regression was analyzed through the lens of the semi-classical bias-variance decomposition introduced in eqn. (3). In our setting,Etest=BS​C+VS​C,subscript𝐸testsubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶subscript𝑉𝑆𝐶E_{\text{test}}=B_{SC}+V_{SC}\,,(23)where,BS​C=𝔼𝐱​𝔼P​X​(𝔼𝜺​[y^​(𝐱)|P,X]−y​(𝐱))2,andVS​C=𝔼𝐱​𝔼P​X​[𝕍𝜺​[y^|P,X]|𝐱].formulae-sequencesubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶subscript𝔼𝐱subscript𝔼𝑃𝑋superscriptsubscript𝔼𝜺delimited-[]conditional^𝑦𝐱𝑃𝑋𝑦𝐱2andsubscript𝑉𝑆𝐶subscript𝔼𝐱subscript𝔼𝑃𝑋delimited-[]conditionalsubscript𝕍𝜺delimited-[]conditional^𝑦𝑃𝑋𝐱B_{SC}=\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{PX}\left({\mathbb{E}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}[\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})|P,X]-y(\mathbf{x})}\right)^{2}\,,\quad\text{and}\quad V_{SC}=\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{PX}[\mathbb{V}_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}[\hat{y}|P,X]|\mathbf{x}]\,.(24)

To gain further insight into this decomposition, we can express BS​Csubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶B_{SC} and VS​Csubscript𝑉𝑆𝐶V_{SC} in terms of the variables in Thm. 1:BS​C=B+VP+VX+VP​X,andVS​C=V𝜺+VP​𝜺+VX​𝜺+VP​X​𝜺.formulae-sequencesubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶𝐵subscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋andsubscript𝑉𝑆𝐶subscript𝑉𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝜺subscript𝑉𝑋𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺B_{SC}=B+V_{P}+V_{X}+V_{PX}\,,\quad\text{and}\quad V_{SC}=V_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}+V_{P\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}+V_{X\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}+V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}\,.(25)Using the correspondence between the variance terms and areas mentioned in Remark 1, we illustrate this decomposition in Fig. 1(a). The figure shows that BS​Csubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶B_{SC} is partially comprised of variance terms. Thm. 1 allows us to exactly characterize how BS​Csubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶B_{SC} and VS​Csubscript𝑉𝑆𝐶V_{SC} depend on the capacity of the model, with results shown in Fig. 1(f). As in [23], we observe that the bias BS​Csubscript𝐵𝑆𝐶B_{SC} and variance VS​Csubscript𝑉𝑆𝐶V_{SC} exhibit nonmonotonic behavior with respect to the model size and both diverge at the interpolation threshold.

Because V𝜺=VP​𝜺=0subscript𝑉𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝜺0V_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}=V_{P\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}=0 and VX​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑋𝜺V_{X\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}} and VP​X​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}} both vanish in the noiseless setting, the semi-classical decomposition has the nice property that VS​C=0subscript𝑉𝑆𝐶0V_{SC}=0 when there is no label noise. However, it is hard to reconcile the nonmonotonicity of the bias with its desired interpretation as a measure of the erroneous assumptions in the model as the latter are expected to decrease as the model increases in capacity. For this reason, we believe the multivariate approach outlined in Sec. 2.2 provides a more interpretable basis for understanding double descent.

Neal et al. [25] adopt the multivariate approach of Sec. 2.2 and decompose the test loss in terms of two sources of randomness, the optimization/initial parameters P𝑃P and data sampling D𝐷D. The total variance is additionally decomposed according to the law of total variance:V=𝔼𝐱​𝕍D​[𝔼P​[y^|D]|𝐱]⏟VD+𝔼𝐱​𝔼D​[𝕍P​[y^|D]|𝐱]⏟VD𝖼,𝑉subscript⏟subscript𝔼𝐱subscript𝕍𝐷delimited-[]conditionalsubscript𝔼𝑃delimited-[]conditional^𝑦𝐷𝐱subscript𝑉𝐷subscript⏟subscript𝔼𝐱subscript𝔼𝐷delimited-[]conditionalsubscript𝕍𝑃delimited-[]conditional^𝑦𝐷𝐱subscriptsuperscript𝑉𝖼𝐷V=\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{V}_{D}[\mathbb{E}_{P}[\hat{y}|D]|\mathbf{x}]}_{V_{D}}+\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{D}[\mathbb{V}_{P}[\hat{y}|D]|\mathbf{x}]}_{V^{\mathsf{c}}_{D}}\,,(26)where Neal et al. [25] suggests an interpretation for the two terms as “variance due to sampling” and “variance due to optimization,” respectively. While the expressions in eqn. (26) are themselves unambiguous, we will see that attributing such an interpretation to them can be somewhat misleading.

Some simple algebra allows us to express VDcsuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝐷cV_{D}^{\textsf{c}} in terms of the terms in Thm. 1 asVDc=VP+VP​X+VP​𝜺+VP​X​𝜺.superscriptsubscript𝑉𝐷csubscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋subscript𝑉𝑃𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V_{D}^{\textsf{c}}=V_{P}+V_{PX}+V_{P\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}+V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}\,.(27)Because eqn. (27) contains VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX} and VP​X​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}, Corollary 1 implies that VDcsuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝐷cV_{D}^{\textsf{c}} diverges at the interpolation threshold, and indeed we observe that in Fig. 1(g). From the above interpretation of the meaning of VDcsuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝐷cV_{D}^{\textsf{c}}, we might therefore conclude that the “variance due to optimization” is the source of double descent.

On the other hand, we could have equally well decided to decompose the variance by conditioning on P𝑃P instead of D𝐷D, yielding,V=𝔼𝐱​𝕍P​[𝔼D​[y^|P]|𝐱]⏟VP+𝔼𝐱​𝔼P​[𝕍D​[y^|P]|𝐱]⏟VP𝖼.𝑉subscript⏟subscript𝔼𝐱subscript𝕍𝑃delimited-[]conditionalsubscript𝔼𝐷delimited-[]conditional^𝑦𝑃𝐱subscript𝑉𝑃subscript⏟subscript𝔼𝐱subscript𝔼𝑃delimited-[]conditionalsubscript𝕍𝐷delimited-[]conditional^𝑦𝑃𝐱subscriptsuperscript𝑉𝖼𝑃V=\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{V}_{P}[\mathbb{E}_{D}[\hat{y}|P]|\mathbf{x}]}_{V_{P}}+\underbrace{\mathbb{E}_{\mathbf{x}}\mathbb{E}_{P}[\mathbb{V}_{D}[\hat{y}|P]|\mathbf{x}]}_{V^{\mathsf{c}}_{P}}\,.(28)The corresponding interpretations of these terms would then be “variance due to optimization” and “variance due to sampling,” respectively. As above, it is straightforward to express VP𝖼subscriptsuperscript𝑉𝖼𝑃V^{\mathsf{c}}_{P} as,VP𝖼=VX+VP​X+VX​𝜺+VP​X​𝜺.subscriptsuperscript𝑉𝖼𝑃subscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋subscript𝑉𝑋𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V^{\mathsf{c}}_{P}=V_{X}+V_{PX}+V_{X\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}+V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}\,.(29)In this case, Corollary 1 implies that VPcsuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝑃cV_{P}^{\textsf{c}} diverges at the interpolation threshold, as Fig. 1(h) confirms. In this case, we might therefore conclude that the “variance due to sampling” is the source of double descent.

The above analysis reveals conflicting explanations for the source double descent, depending on which source of randomness is conditioned on when applying the law of total variance. We believe this ambiguity is undesirable and provides further motivation for the symmetric variance decomposition in Prop. 1.

In the previous two-variable setting, the symmetric decomposition can be written as (see Example 1),V=VP+VD+VP​D.𝑉subscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑉𝐷subscript𝑉𝑃𝐷V=V_{P}+V_{D}+V_{PD}\,.(30)See Fig. 1(d) for an illustration of this decomposition. This figure shows that VP​Dsubscript𝑉𝑃𝐷V_{PD} inhabits the ambiguous overlap region that was responsible for the inconsistent interpretations arising from a naive application of the law of total variance. From the theoretical results shown in Fig. 1(i), it is clear that neither the variance explained by the parameters, VPsubscript𝑉𝑃V_{P}, nor the variance explained by the data, VDsubscript𝑉𝐷V_{D}, can be responsible for double descent; instead it must be VP​Dsubscript𝑉𝑃𝐷V_{PD} that is causing the divergence. Recalling the definition of VP​Dsubscript𝑉𝑃𝐷V_{PD} in Ex. 1, we conclude that the divergence at the interpolation boundary is caused by “the variance explained by the parameters and training data together beyond what they explain individually.”

One implication of this interpretation is that if we had a way of removing either the variance from the parameters or the variance from the data, then the divergence would be eliminated. We examine this phenomenon from the perspective of ensemble and bagging methods in Sec. 5 and confirm empirically that this is indeed the case. See Fig. 2.

Returning to the full model from Sec. 3 with three sources of randomness, we know from Thm. 1 thatV=VP+VX+VP​X+VX​𝜺+VP​X​𝜺,𝑉subscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋subscript𝑉𝑋𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V=V_{P}+V_{X}+V_{PX}+V_{X\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}+V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}\,,(31)while the other two variance terms V𝜺subscript𝑉𝜺V_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}} and VP​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝜺V_{P\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}} vanish. The seven variance terms are illustrated in Fig. 1(e). The dependence of the five non-zero terms on the model’s capacity is plotted in Fig. 1(j). We find that VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX} and VP​X​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}} both diverge at the interpolation threshold while the other terms remain finite. This result helps explain recent empirical results that have found that label noise amplifies the double descent phenomena [13]: because VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX} itself diverges, there is double descent even without label noise, but because VP​X​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}} also diverges, label noise can exacerbate the effect.

The understanding we have developed for the sources of variance enables explicit prediction of the effectiveness of ensemble and bagging techniques.We consider averaging the predictive functions of several independently initialized base learners as well as bagging the predictions from models with independent samples of training data. Specifically, we consider kPsubscript𝑘𝑃k_{P} independent samples of the parameters, Pisubscript𝑃𝑖P_{i}, and kDsubscript𝑘𝐷k_{D} independent samples of the training data, Xjsubscript𝑋𝑗X_{j} and εjsubscript𝜀𝑗{\varepsilon}_{j}. Then our predictive function on a test point 𝐱𝐱\mathbf{x} isy^∗(𝐱):=1kP​kD∑i,jy^i​j(𝐱),\hat{y}^{*}(\mathbf{x})\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\frac{1}{k_{P}k_{D}}\sum_{i,j}\hat{y}_{ij}(\mathbf{x})\,,(32)where the indicies of y^^𝑦\hat{y} indicate the specific sample of parameters and training data used to construct the predictor. A simple calculation gives the variance decomposition of y^∗superscript^𝑦\hat{y}^{*} asVP∗superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑃\displaystyle V_{P}^{*}=VPkP,VX∗=VXkD,Vε∗=VεkD,VX​ε∗=VX​εkD,formulae-sequenceabsentsubscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑘𝑃formulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑘𝐷formulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝜀subscript𝑉𝜀subscript𝑘𝐷superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑋𝜀subscript𝑉𝑋𝜀subscript𝑘𝐷\displaystyle=\frac{V_{P}}{k_{P}},~{}V_{X}^{*}=\frac{V_{X}}{k_{D}},~{}V_{\varepsilon}^{*}=\frac{V_{\varepsilon}}{k_{D}},~{}V_{X{\varepsilon}}^{*}=\frac{V_{X{\varepsilon}}}{k_{D}},(33)VP​ε∗superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑃𝜀\displaystyle V_{P{\varepsilon}}^{*}=VP​εkP​kD,VP​X∗=VP​XkP​kD, and ​VP​X​ε∗=VP​X​εkP​kD,formulae-sequenceabsentsubscript𝑉𝑃𝜀subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷formulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷 and superscriptsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷\displaystyle=\frac{V_{P{\varepsilon}}}{k_{P}k_{D}},~{}V_{PX}^{*}=\frac{V_{PX}}{k_{P}k_{D}},\text{ and }V_{PX{\varepsilon}}^{*}=\frac{V_{PX{\varepsilon}}}{k_{P}k_{D}}\,,(34)while the bias remains the same. We illustrate these results empirically in Fig. 2 and show that ensembles of base learners and bagging are both able to independently reduce the divergence around the interpolation threshold, as they reduce the divergent terms VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX} and VP​X​εsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀V_{PX{\varepsilon}}.

As the computation of eqn. (32) requires evaluating kP​kDsubscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷k_{P}k_{D} base learners, it is natural to try to characterize the optimal combination of ensembles and bagging given a fixed computational budget. We find the optimal ratio is given as[kD/kP]optimal=(VX+Vε+VX​ε)/VP.subscriptdelimited-[]subscript𝑘𝐷subscript𝑘𝑃optimalsubscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑉𝜀subscript𝑉𝑋𝜀subscript𝑉𝑃\left[{k_{D}/k_{P}}\right]_{\text{optimal}}=(V_{X}+V_{{\varepsilon}}+V_{X{\varepsilon}})/V_{P}\,.(35)See Fig. 2, which shows that, for the kernel regression problem studied here, ensembles are typically more efficient at small width and bagging is more efficient at large width.

We analyzed the bias and variance trade-off in the modern setting, where the difference to the classical picture of under- and overfitting is marked. We argued that understanding the behavior of the bias and variance in learning algorithms that depend on large sources of randomness requires rethinking the classical definitions to encompass these sources.

We presented a bias-variance decomposition that is suitable for these settings, and showed how it can help attribute components of the loss to their causes, while avoiding counterintuitive or ambiguous conclusions. For random feature kernel regression, we gave exact predictions for all of the terms in the decomposition and proved that the bias is monotonically decreasing and identified the source of divergence at the interpolation threshold to be the interaction between the noise from sampling and initialization. We showed that while label noise does not cause the divergence, it can exacerbate the effect. Finally, we made exact predictions for ensemble learning and bagging and provided the computationally optimal strategy to combine them.

The law of total variance for two random variables X𝑋X and Y𝑌Y is𝕍​[Y]=𝔼​𝕍​[Y|X]+𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X],𝕍delimited-[]𝑌𝔼𝕍delimited-[]conditional𝑌𝑋𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌𝑋\mathbb{V}[Y]=\mathbb{E}\mathbb{V}[Y|X]+\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X],(S1)where the two terms represents the variance of Y𝑌Y that is unexplained and explained by X𝑋X respectively. Since the variance must be nonnegative it is possible to interpret it as an area. In Fig. 1(a), the total variance is represented by the square, which is in turn broken up into the explained variance (red circle) and unexplained variance (area outside of the circle).

It is possible to extend this idea to several variables. An observation that is key to the interpretation is𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X1]+𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X2]≤𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X1,X2],𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋1𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋2𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋1subscript𝑋2\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{1}]+\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{2}]\leq\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{1},X_{2}],(S2)i.e. the “variance explained” is a superadditive function. So the decomposition for two variables could be written as𝕍​[Y]=𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X1]+𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X2]+(𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X1,X2]−𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X1]−𝕍​𝔼​[Y|X2])+𝔼​𝕍​[Y|X1,X2],𝕍delimited-[]𝑌𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋1𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋2𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋1subscript𝑋2𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋1𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋2𝔼𝕍delimited-[]conditional𝑌subscript𝑋1subscript𝑋2\mathbb{V}[Y]=\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{1}]+\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{2}]+\left({\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{1},X_{2}]-\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{1}]-\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|X_{2}]}\right)+\mathbb{E}\mathbb{V}[Y|X_{1},X_{2}],(S3)with the terms interpreted as the variance explained by X1subscript𝑋1X_{1}, the variance explained by X2subscript𝑋2X_{2}, the additional variance explained by X1subscript𝑋1X_{1} and X2subscript𝑋2X_{2} together, and the variance left unexplained by X1subscript𝑋1X_{1} and X2subscript𝑋2X_{2}. Note that the terms are all guaranteed to be positive by eqn. (S2). See Fig 1(b).

Generalizing, let 𝐗:=(X1,…,Xk)assign𝐗subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑘\mathbf{X}:=(X_{1},\ldots,X_{k}) be a collection of random variables. Consider a Venn diagram of k𝑘k circles, and denote the disjoint areas using V𝐢subscript𝑉𝐢V_{\mathbf{i}} for a vector 𝐢∈{0,1}k𝐢superscript01𝑘\mathbf{i}\in\left\{{0,1}\right\}^{k}, where ijsubscript𝑖𝑗i_{j} indicates whether the area is inside the j𝑗jth circle (see Fig S1). We make use of the natural partial ordering on {0,1}ksuperscript01𝑘\{0,1\}^{k}, i.e. 𝐢≤𝐣𝐢𝐣\mathbf{i}\leq\mathbf{j} if and only if il≤jlsubscript𝑖𝑙subscript𝑗𝑙i_{l}\leq j_{l} for all l𝑙l. Note the ordering indicates the subset relation if the vectors are thought of as indicator vectors. We also use the notation 𝐞jsubscript𝐞𝑗\mathbf{e}_{j} for the standard basis vectors, and define the vectors 𝐗𝐢:=(Xj:ij=1)\mathbf{X}_{\mathbf{i}}:=(X_{j}:i_{j}=1).

For simplicity, assume Y∈σ​(𝐗)𝑌𝜎𝐗Y\in\sigma(\mathbf{X}), so that 𝔼​[f​(Y)|𝐗]=f​(Y)𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑓𝑌𝐗𝑓𝑌\mathbb{E}[f(Y)|\mathbf{X}]=f(Y) for any measurable function f𝑓f and all the variance of Y𝑌Y is explained by 𝐗𝐗\mathbf{X}, i.e.𝕍​𝔼​[Y|𝐗]=𝕍​[Y]𝕍𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑌𝐗𝕍delimited-[]𝑌\mathbb{V}\mathbb{E}[Y|\mathbf{X}]=\mathbb{V}[Y](S4)or V𝟎=0subscript𝑉00V_{\mathbf{0}}=0. To calculate the variances terms used above for our model, we use a coupling: we introduce a copy of the underlying random variables 𝐗𝐗\mathbf{X} and take expectations under different independence assumptions on 𝐗𝐗\mathbf{X} and its copy. The idea is similar to the proof of Lemma S2, and indeed this generalize the result. First we prove eqn. (S2) to illustrate the idea with simple notation. To find the optimal ratio, we write the test error asB+VPkP+VXkD+VεkD+VX​εkD+VP​XkP​kD+VP​εkP​kD+VP​X​εkP​kD𝐵subscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑉𝑋subscript𝑘𝐷subscript𝑉𝜀subscript𝑘𝐷subscript𝑉𝑋𝜀subscript𝑘𝐷subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷subscript𝑉𝑃𝜀subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀subscript𝑘𝑃subscript𝑘𝐷B+\frac{V_{P}}{k_{P}}+\frac{V_{X}}{k_{D}}+\frac{V_{\varepsilon}}{k_{D}}+\frac{V_{X{\varepsilon}}}{k_{D}}+\frac{V_{PX}}{k_{P}k_{D}}+\frac{V_{P{\varepsilon}}}{k_{P}k_{D}}+\frac{V_{PX{\varepsilon}}}{k_{P}k_{D}}(S31)and substitute kD=K/kPsubscript𝑘𝐷𝐾subscript𝑘𝑃k_{D}=K/k_{P}, where K𝐾K is a fixed constant. Then differentiating eqn. (S31) with respect to kPsubscript𝑘𝑃k_{P} and solving for the stationary point yields eqn. (35).

Following [24], we consider the task of learning an unknown function from m𝑚m independent samples (𝐱i,yi)∈ℝn0×ℝ,i≤mformulae-sequencesubscript𝐱𝑖subscript𝑦𝑖superscriptℝsubscript𝑛0ℝ𝑖𝑚(\mathbf{x}_{i},y_{i})\in\mathbb{R}^{n_{0}}\times\mathbb{R},\,i\leq m, where the datapoints are standard Gaussian, 𝐱i∼𝒩​(0,In0)similar-tosubscript𝐱𝑖𝒩0subscript𝐼subscript𝑛0\mathbf{x}_{i}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,I_{n_{0}}), and the labels are generated by a wide444We assume the width nt→∞→subscript𝑛tn_{\textsc{t}}\to\infty, but the rate is not important. single-hidden-layer neural network:yi|𝐱i,Ω,ω∼ω​σt​(Ω​𝐱i/n0)/nt+εi.similar-toconditionalsubscript𝑦𝑖subscript𝐱𝑖Ω𝜔𝜔subscript𝜎tΩsubscript𝐱𝑖subscript𝑛0subscript𝑛tsubscript𝜀𝑖y_{i}|\mathbf{x}_{i},\Omega,\omega\sim\omega\sigma_{\textsc{t}}(\Omega\mathbf{x}_{i}/\sqrt{n_{0}})/\sqrt{n_{\textsc{t}}}+\varepsilon_{i}\,.(S32)The teacher’s activation function σtsubscript𝜎t\sigma_{\textsc{t}} is applied coordinate-wise, and its parameters Ω∈ℝnt×n0Ωsuperscriptℝsubscript𝑛tsubscript𝑛0\Omega\in\mathbb{R}^{n_{\textsc{t}}\times n_{0}} and ω∈ℝ1×nt𝜔superscriptℝ1subscript𝑛t\omega\in\mathbb{R}^{1\times n_{\textsc{t}}} are matrices whose entries are independently sampled once for all data from 𝒩​(0,1)𝒩01\mathcal{N}(0,1). Recall that in our high-dimensional asymptotics the limiting ratios n0/m→ϕ→subscript𝑛0𝑚italic-ϕn_{0}/m\to\phi and n0/n1→ψ→subscript𝑛0subscript𝑛1𝜓n_{0}/n_{1}\to\psi are constant.As we will discuss in Sec. S3, in this regime only linear functions of the data can be learned, a finding that is consistent with observations made in [38, 23]. When the teacher width nt→∞→subscript𝑛tn_{\textsc{t}}\to\infty, a precise decomposition of the teacher emerges that neatly captures its learning and unlearnable components. Specifically, if we define,ζt:=(𝔼σt′(g))2,andηt:=𝔼σt(g)2,\zeta_{\textsc{t}}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=(\mathbb{E}\sigma_{\textsc{t}}^{\prime}(g))^{2}\,,\;\;\;\,\text{and}\;\;\;\,\eta_{\textsc{t}}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\mathbb{E}\sigma_{\textsc{t}}(g)^{2}\,,(S34)then there is an equivalent linear teacher plus noise with signal-to-noise ratio given by,SNR=ζt/(ηt−ζt+σ𝜺2).SNRsubscript𝜁tsubscript𝜂tsubscript𝜁tsuperscriptsubscript𝜎𝜺2\text{SNR}={\zeta_{\textsc{t}}}/\left({\eta_{\textsc{t}}-\zeta_{\textsc{t}}+\sigma_{\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}^{2}}\right)\,.(S35)We often make this equivalence to a linear teacher explicit by setting σt​(x)=xsubscript𝜎t𝑥𝑥\sigma_{\textsc{t}}(x)=x (which implies ηt=ζt=1subscript𝜂tsubscript𝜁t1\eta_{\textsc{t}}=\zeta_{\textsc{t}}=1) and explicitly adding label noise σε2=1/SNRsuperscriptsubscript𝜎𝜀21SNR\sigma_{\varepsilon}^{2}=1/\text{SNR}. This procedure also removes the noise from the test label, but since this noise merely contributes an additive shift to the test loss, removing it does not change any of our conclusions.

We consider predictive functions y^^𝑦\hat{y} defined by approximate (i.e. random feature) kernel ridge regression using the NTK of a single-hidden-layer neural network of width n1subscript𝑛1n_{1} with entry-wise activation function σ𝜎\sigma, defined by,N0​(𝐱)=W2​σ​(W1​𝐱/n0)/n1,subscript𝑁0𝐱subscript𝑊2𝜎subscript𝑊1𝐱subscript𝑛0subscript𝑛1N_{0}(\mathbf{x})=W_{2}\sigma(W_{1}\mathbf{x}/\sqrt{n_{0}})/\sqrt{n_{1}}\,,(S36)for initial n1×n0subscript𝑛1subscript𝑛0n_{1}\times n_{0} and 1×n11subscript𝑛11\times n_{1} weight matrices with iid entries [W1]i​j∼𝒩​(0,1)similar-tosubscriptdelimited-[]subscript𝑊1𝑖𝑗𝒩01[W_{1}]_{ij}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,1)555Any non-zero σW12superscriptsubscript𝜎subscript𝑊12\sigma_{W_{1}}^{2} can be absorbed into a redefinition of σ𝜎\sigma. and [W2]i∼𝒩​(0,σW22)similar-tosubscriptdelimited-[]subscript𝑊2𝑖𝒩0superscriptsubscript𝜎subscript𝑊22[W_{2}]_{i}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,\sigma_{W_{2}}^{2}).

The NTK can be considered a kernel K𝐾K that is approximated by random features corresponding to the Jacobian J𝐽J of the network’s output with respect to its parameters, i.e. K​(𝐱1,𝐱2)=J​(𝐱1)​J​(𝐱2)⊤𝐾subscript𝐱1subscript𝐱2𝐽subscript𝐱1𝐽superscriptsubscript𝐱2topK(\mathbf{x}_{1},\mathbf{x}_{2})=J(\mathbf{x}_{1})J(\mathbf{x}_{2})^{\top}. The Jacobian itself naturally decomposes into the Jacobian with respect to W1subscript𝑊1W_{1} and W2subscript𝑊2W_{2}, i.e. J​(𝐱)=[∂N0​(𝐱)/∂W1,∂N0​(𝐱)/∂W2]=[J1​(𝐱),J2​(𝐱)]𝐽𝐱subscript𝑁0𝐱subscript𝑊1subscript𝑁0𝐱subscript𝑊2subscript𝐽1𝐱subscript𝐽2𝐱J(\mathbf{x})=[\partial N_{0}(\mathbf{x})/\partial W_{1},\partial N_{0}(\mathbf{x})/\partial W_{2}]=[J_{1}(\mathbf{x}),J_{2}(\mathbf{x})]. Therefore the kernel K𝐾K also decomposes this way, and we can write.K(𝐱1,𝐱2)=J1(𝐱1)J1(𝐱2)⊤+J2(𝐱1)J2(𝐱2)⊤=:K1(𝐱1,𝐱2)+K2(𝐱1,𝐱2).K(\mathbf{x}_{1},\mathbf{x}_{2})=\;J_{1}(\mathbf{x}_{1})J_{1}(\mathbf{x}_{2})^{\top}+J_{2}(\mathbf{x}_{1})J_{2}(\mathbf{x}_{2})^{\top}=\mathrel{\mathop{:}}\;K_{1}(\mathbf{x}_{1},\mathbf{x}_{2})+K_{2}(\mathbf{x}_{1},\mathbf{x}_{2}).(S37)As the width of the network becomes very large (compared to all other relevant scales in the system), the approximate NTK converges to a constant kernel determined by the network’s initial parameters and describes the trajectory of the network’s output under gradient descent.666If the width is not asymptotically larger than the dataset size, the kernel system may not accurately describe the late-time predictions of the neural network. In this work, we focus on the predictive function defined by the solution to this kernel regression problem,y^(𝐱):=N0(𝐱)+(Y−N0(X))K−1K𝐱\hat{y}(\mathbf{x})\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=N_{0}(\mathbf{x})+(Y-N_{0}(X))K^{-1}K_{\mathbf{x}}\,(S38)for K:=K(X,X)+γImK\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=K(X,X)+\gamma I_{m}, K𝐱:=K(X,𝐱)K_{\mathbf{x}}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=K(X,\mathbf{x}), and γ𝛾\gamma is a ridge regularization constant. A simple calculation yields the per-layer constituent kernels,K1​(𝐱1,𝐱2)subscript𝐾1subscript𝐱1subscript𝐱2\displaystyle K_{1}(\mathbf{x}_{1},\mathbf{x}_{2})=X⊤​Xn0⊙(F′)⊤diag(W2)2F′n1and\displaystyle=\frac{X^{\top}X}{n_{0}}\odot\frac{\left({F^{\prime}}\right)^{\top}\operatorname{diag}(W_{2})^{2}F^{\prime}}{n_{1}}\quad\text{and}(S39)K2​(𝐱1,𝐱2)subscript𝐾2subscript𝐱1subscript𝐱2\displaystyle K_{2}(\mathbf{x}_{1},\mathbf{x}_{2})=1n1​F⊤​F,absent1subscript𝑛1superscript𝐹top𝐹\displaystyle=\frac{1}{n_{1}}F^{\top}F\,,(S40)where we have introduced the abbreviations F:=σ(W1X/n0)F\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\sigma(W_{1}X/\sqrt{n_{0}}) and F′:=σ′(W1X/n0)F^{\prime}\mathrel{\mathop{:}}=\sigma^{\prime}(W_{1}X/\sqrt{n_{0}}). Notice that when σW22→0→superscriptsubscript𝜎subscript𝑊220\sigma_{W_{2}}^{2}\to 0, K=K2𝐾subscript𝐾2K=K_{2}, i.e. the NTK degenerates into the standard random features kernel of the main text.

The predictive function (S38) contains an offset N0​(𝐱)subscript𝑁0𝐱N_{0}(\mathbf{x}) which would typically be set to zero in standard random feature kernel regression because it simply increases the variance of test predictions. Removing this variance component has an analogous operation in neural network training: either the function value at initialization can be subtracted throughout training, or a symmetrization trick can be used in which two copies of the neural network are initialized identically, and their normalized difference N≡(N(a)−N(b))/2𝑁superscript𝑁𝑎superscript𝑁𝑏2N\equiv\left({N^{(a)}-N^{(b)}}\right)/{\sqrt{2}} is trained with gradient descent. Either method preserves the kernel K𝐾K while enforcing N0≡0subscript𝑁00N_{0}\equiv 0. We call this procedure centering, and present results with and without it.

Finally, we note that ridge regularization in the kernel perspective corresponds to using L2 regularization of the neural network’s weights toward their initial values.

Here we state a generalization of the results from Sec. 3.1 to the NTK. As discussed above, the results for random feature kernel regression follow by setting σW2=0subscript𝜎subscript𝑊20\sigma_{W_{2}}=0. The proofs are presented in the subsequent sections.

We consider the limiting behavior of tracial expressions as the dimensions in our model diverge to infinity as their ratios are held fixed according to ϕitalic-ϕ\phi and ψ𝜓\psi. The tracial expressions are random variables that converge in probability to deterministic constants, which are specified as the solution to a coupled equation defined below.

We briefly highlight some results for the full version of Theorem S3 that are distinct from the special case Theorem 1. Note that since the model in eqn. (S38) corresponds to the full NTK, the model has n1​(n0+1)subscript𝑛1subscript𝑛01n_{1}(n_{0}+1) parameters. Thus n1=msubscript𝑛1𝑚n_{1}=m does not occur at the interpolation threshold but instead represents a significantly overparameterized model. Previous work has found nonmonotonic behavior in the test loss for the model in eqn. (S38) at both the interpolation threshold and when n1=msubscript𝑛1𝑚n_{1}=m [24]. Since n1=msubscript𝑛1𝑚n_{1}=m is far beyond the interpolation threshold, this second occurrence of nonmonotonicity is qualitatively different than double descent behavior. Our variance decomposition sheds light on the source of this second occurrence of nonmonotonic behavior (see Fig. S2).

We find that none of the variance terms are divergent, but the sources of the nonmonotonicity are VPsubscript𝑉𝑃V_{P}, VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX}, and VP​X​εsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀V_{PX{\varepsilon}}. Curiously, bagging this predictive function for a large number of dataset samples would remove all other sources of variance except VPsubscript𝑉𝑃V_{P}. This would have the effect of highlighting the nonmonotonicity in the total variance.

Even though the individual error terms E21,E22,E31,E32,E33subscript𝐸21subscript𝐸22subscript𝐸31subscript𝐸32subscript𝐸33E_{21},E_{22},E_{31},E_{32},E_{33} can be written as the trace of self-adjoint matrices, the individual Q𝑄Q matrices are not themselves self-adjoint. However, by enlarging the dimensionality by a factor of two, equivalent self-adjoint representations can easily be constructed. To do so, we simply utilize the identity,UT​Q​V=U¯⊤​Q¯​V¯≡(12​U⊤V⊤)​(0Q⊤Q0)​(12​UV).superscript𝑈𝑇𝑄𝑉superscript¯𝑈top¯𝑄¯𝑉matrix12superscript𝑈topsuperscript𝑉topmatrix0superscript𝑄top𝑄0matrix12𝑈𝑉U^{T}QV=\bar{U}^{\top}\bar{Q}\bar{V}\equiv\begin{pmatrix}\frac{1}{2}U^{\top}&V^{\top}\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}0&Q^{\top}\\Q&0\end{pmatrix}\begin{pmatrix}\frac{1}{2}U\\V\end{pmatrix}\,.(S144)Observe that Q¯21,Q¯22,Q¯31,Q¯32subscript¯𝑄21subscript¯𝑄22subscript¯𝑄31subscript¯𝑄32\bar{Q}_{21},\bar{Q}_{22},\bar{Q}_{31},\bar{Q}_{32} and Q¯33subscript¯𝑄33\bar{Q}_{33} are all self-adjoint block matrices whose blocks are either constants or proportional to one of {X,X⊤,W1,W1⊤,ΘF,ΘF⊤}𝑋superscript𝑋topsubscript𝑊1superscriptsubscript𝑊1topsubscriptΘ𝐹superscriptsubscriptΘ𝐹top\{X,X^{\top},W_{1},W_{1}^{\top},\Theta_{F},\Theta_{F}^{\top}\}; let us denote the constant terms as Z𝑍Z. As such, we can directly utilize the results of [31, 40] to compute the error terms in question.

For each linear pencil, the corresponding error term can be extracted from the operator-valued Stieltjes transform G:Md​(ℂ)+→Md​(ℂ)+:𝐺→subscript𝑀𝑑superscriptℂsubscript𝑀𝑑superscriptℂG:M_{d}(\mathbb{C})^{+}\to M_{d}(\mathbb{C})^{+}, which is a solution of the equation,Z​G=Id+η​(G)​G,𝑍𝐺subscript𝐼𝑑𝜂𝐺𝐺ZG=I_{d}+\eta(G)G\,,(S145)where d𝑑d is the number of blocks, η:Md​(ℂ)→Md​(ℂ):𝜂→subscript𝑀𝑑ℂsubscript𝑀𝑑ℂ\eta:M_{d}(\mathbb{C})\to M_{d}(\mathbb{C}) defined by[η​(D)]i​j=∑k​lσ​(i,k;l,j)​αk​Dk​l,subscriptdelimited-[]𝜂𝐷𝑖𝑗subscript𝑘𝑙𝜎𝑖𝑘𝑙𝑗subscript𝛼𝑘subscript𝐷𝑘𝑙[\eta(D)]_{ij}=\sum_{kl}\sigma(i,k;l,j)\alpha_{k}D_{kl}\,,(S146)where αksubscript𝛼𝑘\alpha_{k} is dimensionality of the k𝑘kth block and σ​(i,k;l,k)𝜎𝑖𝑘𝑙𝑘\sigma(i,k;l,k) denotes the covariance between the entries of the i​j𝑖𝑗ij block of Q¯¯𝑄\bar{Q} and entries of the k​l𝑘𝑙kl block of Q¯¯𝑄\bar{Q}. Eqn. (S145) may admit many solutions, but there is a unique solution such that Im​G≻0succeedsIm𝐺0\text{Im}G\succ 0 for Im​Z≻0succeedsIm𝑍0\text{Im}Z\succ 0.

The constants Z𝑍Z, the entries of σ𝜎\sigma, and therefore the equations (S146) are manifest by inspection of the block matrix representations for Q𝑄Q. Although the matrix representations are too large to reproduce here, we can nevertheless extract the equations satisfied by each entry of G𝐺G, which we present in the subsequent sections.

Therefore, we find for ϕ>ψitalic-ϕ𝜓\phi>\psi the same behavior as for ϕ<ψitalic-ϕ𝜓\phi<\psi, namely,τ2τ1=τ2′τ1′=𝒪​(1),τ1′τ12=𝒪​(1ϕ−ψ),τ2′τ12=𝒪​(1ϕ−ψ).formulae-sequencesubscript𝜏2subscript𝜏1superscriptsubscript𝜏2′superscriptsubscript𝜏1′𝒪1formulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript𝜏1′superscriptsubscript𝜏12𝒪1italic-ϕ𝜓superscriptsubscript𝜏2′superscriptsubscript𝜏12𝒪1italic-ϕ𝜓\frac{\tau_{2}}{\tau_{1}}=\frac{\tau_{2}^{\prime}}{\tau_{1}^{\prime}}=\mathcal{O}(1)\,,\quad\frac{\tau_{1}^{\prime}}{\tau_{1}^{2}}=\mathcal{O}\left(\frac{1}{\phi-\psi}\right)\,,\quad\frac{\tau_{2}^{\prime}}{\tau_{1}^{2}}=\mathcal{O}\left(\frac{1}{\phi-\psi}\right)\,.(S313)Altogether, we conclude that as ϕ→ψ→italic-ϕ𝜓\phi\to\psi,B=𝒪​(1),VP=𝒪​(1),VX=𝒪​(1),VX​𝜺=𝒪​(1),VP​X=𝒪​(1ϕ−ψ),VP​X​𝜺=𝒪​(1ϕ−ψ),formulae-sequence𝐵𝒪1formulae-sequencesubscript𝑉𝑃𝒪1formulae-sequencesubscript𝑉𝑋𝒪1formulae-sequencesubscript𝑉𝑋𝜺𝒪1formulae-sequencesubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝒪1italic-ϕ𝜓subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺𝒪1italic-ϕ𝜓B=\mathcal{O}(1)\,,\quad V_{P}=\mathcal{O}(1)\,,\quad V_{X}=\mathcal{O}(1)\,,\quad V_{X\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}=\mathcal{O}(1)\,,\quad V_{PX}=\mathcal{O}\left(\frac{1}{\phi-\psi}\right)\,,\quad V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}=\mathcal{O}\left(\frac{1}{\phi-\psi}\right)\,,(S314)i.e. the only divergent terms are VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX} and VP​X​𝜺subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜺V_{PX\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}}.

While finalizing this manuscript, we became aware of a related work [35] that similarly proposes and calculates a multivariate variance decomposition in order to examine the origins of double descent. Their approach is sequential in nature, first defining ℰNoisesubscriptℰNoise\mathcal{E}_{\text{Noise}} to be the (expected) variance conditional on P𝑃P and X𝑋X, then ℰInitsubscriptℰInit\mathcal{E}_{\text{Init}} to be the remaining variance conditional on X𝑋X, and finally ℰSampsubscriptℰSamp\mathcal{E}_{\text{Samp}} to be the remaining variance. In terms of our fine-grained decomposition, their expressions read,ℰBias=B,ℰInit=VP+VP​X,ℰSamp=VX,ℰNoise=VP​X​ε+VXε+VP​ε+Vε.formulae-sequencesubscriptℰBias𝐵formulae-sequencesubscriptℰInitsubscript𝑉𝑃subscript𝑉𝑃𝑋formulae-sequencesubscriptℰSampsubscript𝑉𝑋subscriptℰNoisesubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀subscript𝑉subscript𝑋𝜀subscript𝑉𝑃𝜀subscript𝑉𝜀\mathcal{E}_{\text{Bias}}=B\,,\quad\mathcal{E}_{\text{Init}}=V_{P}+V_{PX}\,,\quad\mathcal{E}_{\text{Samp}}=V_{X}\,,\quad\mathcal{E}_{\text{Noise}}=V_{PX\varepsilon}+V_{X_{\varepsilon}}+V_{P\varepsilon}+V_{\varepsilon}\,.(S315)Fig. S3(a) illustrates their decomposition in terms of a Venn diagram and Fig. S3(b) shows how the components of their decomposition behave as the number of random features varies, similarly to Figs. 1 and S2. Note that their total bias and total variance agree with ours, and that their decomposition also resolves the two separate divergent terms at the interpolation threshold (since ℰNoisesubscriptℰNoise\mathcal{E}_{\text{Noise}} contains VP​X​εsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀V_{PX{\varepsilon}} and ℰInitsubscriptℰInit\mathcal{E}_{\text{Init}} contains VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX}). However, because their decomposition is not fully multivariate, the resulting areas do not necessarily possess the interpretations one might expect from the names “noise variance," “initialization variance," and “sampling variance." For example, the divergence in ℰNoisesubscriptℰNoise\mathcal{E}_{\text{Noise}} ultimately comes from the contribution of VP​X​εsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀V_{PX\varepsilon}, which vanishes when you ensemble over initial parameters, for example. This strong dependence on the parameters does not seem like a desirable property of a quantity designed to measure the variance due to noise. Similarly, the divergence of ℰInitsubscriptℰInit\mathcal{E}_{\text{Init}} can be eliminated by ensembling (bagging) over different training samples, which also seems like a undesirable property of “initialization variance." The underlying reason for these inconsistent interpretations is that the divergences ultimately arise from the interaction terms VP​Xsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋V_{PX} and VP​X​εsubscript𝑉𝑃𝑋𝜀V_{PX\varepsilon}, but these interactions are not captured in their decomposition.
